    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/407055-does-anyone-else-think-that-the">Does anyone else think that the description of this book is an enormous spoiler? While reading the book it felt like one of the big mysteries being set up in the book was answered in this description. I really think I would have enjoyed it more if I didn&#39;t know the answers to the questions faced by the characters in the first two-thirds of the book.</a>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The summary is used not only on the book, but on Tor Books' page for it, the Amazon listing, and the Barnes & Noble listing.<br/><br/>Since so many people have <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The summary is used not only on the book, but on Tor Books' page for it, the Amazon listing, and the Barnes &amp; Noble listing.<br /><br />Since so many people have reported this, I truncated the original summary and replaced it with a summary/review from Publishers Weekly, which is both more detailed and (I believe) does not include the big spoiler.<br /><br />Thoughts on this change welcome.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This is what I consider hard sci-fi at its very best. Definitely not space opera. Many of the characters are heavy duty theoretical physicists. Read t<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This is what I consider hard sci-fi at its very best. Definitely not space opera. Many of the characters are heavy duty theoretical physicists. Read the <a>NPR review</a>, it's a good one:.<br />"This is hard SF, full of lovingly lengthy passages of technical exposition about everything from quantum mechanics to artificial intelligence. But Cixin Liu supports all of that braintwisting theory with empathetic characters and a strong action-thriller backbone. "<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_1346947138" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1346947138" />
    <a title="Mona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7020601-mona"><img alt="Mona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392557851p2/7020601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1346947138?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mona" href="/user/show/7020601-mona">Mona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1346947138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2479048017807546527"><br/><b>Badly Written and Ill Conceived Science Fiction with a Few Interesting Ideas</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438275782i/15688772._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>I'm not sure I read the same book as everyone else. This got lots of four and five star reviews here on Goodreads. <br/><br/>Plus, it's been nominated for both Hugo and Nebula awards.<br/><br/>As often happens, I'm not with the majority opinion here. I give it two stars.<br/><br/>I'm not sure if the problem was the translation, or the original text, or both. (Unfortunately I only have the audio for this, so I can't quote the text here).<br/><br/>But I found th</span>
  <span id="freeText2479048017807546527" style="display:none"><br><b>Badly Written and Ill Conceived Science Fiction with a Few Interesting Ideas</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438275782i/15688772._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I'm not sure I read the same book as everyone else. This got lots of four and five star reviews here on Goodreads. <br><br>Plus, it's been nominated for both Hugo and Nebula awards.<br><br>As often happens, I'm not with the majority opinion here. I give it two stars.<br><br>I'm not sure if the problem was the translation, or the original text, or both. (Unfortunately I only have the audio for this, so I can't quote the text here).<br><br>But I found the writing wooden, the characters two-dimensional cartoonish stick figures, and the audio narration poor. Although most of the characters are academics or intellectuals, the most believable and interesting character turns out to be a coarse and apparently ignorant policeman, She Qiang (nicknamed "Da Shi").<br><br>Most of the characters are cold and unsympathetic. A few of them commit murders for which they seem entirely unremorseful. It's impossible to care about these people.<br><br>And there are many unbelievable plot developments. For example, Mike Evans, an environmentalist, conveniently inherits his billionaire father's money at just the right time in the plot. I won't say much more about this as it's a spoiler.<br><br>This might have been acceptable in the early years of science fiction, but now it just seems like a bad novel. <br><br>As for the science...I'm not a physicist, so I can't really discuss the physics. Evidently, the three body problem has been unsolved by physicists dating back to Newton. So this part has some scientific basis. <br><br>Also, the stuff about micro circuitry was interesting.<br><br>But I have worked with computers for many years. The "human formation computer" (a computer powered by trained soldiers with colored flags) seems a bit silly to me, although nothing is impossible. I doubt if millions of humans could achieve the required precision. Interestingly, a minor character in the book who is an executive in a software company says the same thing.<br><br>Also, the author seems preoccupied with social status. And science is held up as an object of worship.<br><br>Science and technology are important, but I don't think they should be a religion (which in some quarters they seem to have become).<br><br>Anyway, here's a brief summary. <br><br>As a young girl, Ye Wenjie, an astrophysicist, witnesses the killing of her father by Cultural Revolution fanatics.<br><br>She becomes an astrophysicist herself and is recruited for a secret Chinese science/military project, Red Coast. It's years before she learns the true purpose of the project.<br><br>Meanwhile, many intellectuals are playing a video game (which requires a haptic suit), called "The Three Body Problem". The main gamer character, Wang Miao, is a professor of physics specializing in nanotech.<br><br>It turns out the game and the Red Coast project are connected.<br><br>They both relate to extraterrestrial life.<br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The game transports the players to the Trisolar Universe, a universe with three suns that really exists.<br><br>In most respects, Trisolar is behind earth technologically (they are playing out earlier eras in earth history), but in certain respects they are ahead of earth.<br><br>Trisolar swings between "chaotic eras" and "stable eras". Both of these last for indeterminate time periods. Hundreds of civilizations there are destroyed by extremes of heat and cold produced by configurations of the three suns. Residents of Trisolar dehydrate themselves to survive chaotic eras.<br><br>The game's purpose is to spread information about Trisolar among persons of high intellectual capacity and high social standing. Even though Trisolar really exists, the game plays out differently for different players.<br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Anyway, I won't say much more about the story. I don't want to spoil it.<br><br>If you liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18007564.The_Martian" title="The Martian by Andy Weir" rel="nofollow noopener">The Martian</a>, you might enjoy this. The focus is technology and science, with the prose, characters, and plot being entirely secondary to the ideas. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">Isaac Asimov</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29579.Foundation__Foundation___1_" title="Foundation (Foundation, #1) by Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">Foundation</a> was another book where the characters and plot were subordinate to the ideas, although I think it works much better as a novel than this does.<br><br>I didn't really care for Luke Daniels' audio narration either. His voice varied from leaden in some spots to over-excited in others. And when doing foreign accents, he either exaggerated them, or in some cases, got them wrong. <br><br>I'd say pass on this one, except that lots of others seem to love it. <br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="2479048017807546527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13759575365905057209"><b>PUBLIC ANNOUNCEMENT</b> <br/><br/>I just rated a book 5 stars. <br/><br/>Just read it.<br/><br/>**Update now that I've read the whole trilogy!**<br/><br/>This is now one of my all time favorite sci-fi series. The concepts are brilliant and I fear that I'll never find anything this mind-blowing.<br/><br/>With that said... the books are quite sexist (the first half of the first two books are quite slow and painful) but I still recommend them if you can stomach it.<br/><br/>They're incredibly science heavy but anyone interested in space and first contact with a</span>
  <span id="freeText13759575365905057209" style="display:none"><b>PUBLIC ANNOUNCEMENT</b> <br /><br />I just rated a book 5 stars. <br /><br />Just read it.<br /><br />**Update now that I've read the whole trilogy!**<br /><br />This is now one of my all time favorite sci-fi series. The concepts are brilliant and I fear that I'll never find anything this mind-blowing.<br /><br />With that said... the books are quite sexist (the first half of the first two books are quite slow and painful) but I still recommend them if you can stomach it.<br /><br />They're incredibly science heavy but anyone interested in space and first contact with aliens needs to read this!</span>

<div id="review_1552711376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1552711376" />
    <a title="Rick Riordan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25375513-rick-riordan"><img alt="Rick Riordan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442449448p2/25375513.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1552711376?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick Riordan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick Riordan" href="/user/show/25375513-rick-riordan">Rick Riordan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1552711376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10450113054574748213">Adult sci-fi. By Chinese author Cixin Liu, The Three-Body Problem takes a classic scenario -- contact with alien life -- and cranks up the sinister factor to maximum. The story begins during the Cultural Revolution when young Ye Wenjie watches her scientist father beaten to death by fervent revolutionaries. She is sent off for hard labor at a re-education camp, but by a strange twist of fate gets a chance to work at a top secret government project seeking out extraterrestrial life. Fast forward </span>
  <span id="freeText10450113054574748213" style="display:none">Adult sci-fi. By Chinese author Cixin Liu, The Three-Body Problem takes a classic scenario -- contact with alien life -- and cranks up the sinister factor to maximum. The story begins during the Cultural Revolution when young Ye Wenjie watches her scientist father beaten to death by fervent revolutionaries. She is sent off for hard labor at a re-education camp, but by a strange twist of fate gets a chance to work at a top secret government project seeking out extraterrestrial life. Fast forward to the present, when nanotech scientist Wang Miao is snatched up by cops and brought to a secret meeting of military officials who are fighting an unnamed enemy -- some force that is trying to destroy the roots of human science and technology by killing scientists or driving them to suicide. Wang goes undercover in this strange conspiracy when he started playing a virtual reality game called The Three-Body Problem, which only the most brilliant scientific minds can hope to beat.<br /><br />The premise is fascinating and well-grounded (as far as I can tell) in hard science. The book raises haunting questions: Do we really *want* to contact other civilizations? If you had the chance to pull the plug on the human race, would you do so? Is science truly objective and provable, or is it simply the best we can do given our limited understanding of four dimensions? <br /><br />I found the novel a bit of a struggle until about halfway in. There are a lot of characters, and many of them seem like ciphers to advance the plot or mouthpieces to espouse ideas rather than living breathing people. Sometimes the prose seems like the summary of a novel rather than a novel. However, the ideas are compelling. This is about as close to "mind-blowing" as any book I've read. If you like big ideas and fantasy based on hard science, this is worth a read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10450113054574748213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1118525991" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1118525991" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1118525991?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=top-one-hundred">top-one-hundred</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fanboy-goes-squee">fanboy-goes-squee</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1118525991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17946371469530459163">From the opening, I was struck by how much history I didn't know about China's Cultural Revolution. It might be obvious to anyone growing up in those parts, of course, but I was almost lost in that story long before I saw that there was anything sci-fi about the novel. This is a good thing. It speaks of good writing.<br/><br/>And then things changed. I became a frog in a pot. Small hints accumulate, surrounded by mathematical problems both fundamental and curious. <br/><br/>And then the MC's sanity is questioned. I</span>
  <span id="freeText17946371469530459163" style="display:none">From the opening, I was struck by how much history I didn't know about China's Cultural Revolution. It might be obvious to anyone growing up in those parts, of course, but I was almost lost in that story long before I saw that there was anything sci-fi about the novel. This is a good thing. It speaks of good writing.<br /><br />And then things changed. I became a frog in a pot. Small hints accumulate, surrounded by mathematical problems both fundamental and curious. <br /><br />And then the MC's sanity is questioned. It's an open question that both the reader and the character must answer.<br /><br />And then I got an idea. I could easily make the argument that all scientists in this novel are actually Main Characters, and indeed, that theory only becomes crystal clear later in the novel. It was a delight.<br /><br />The novel is full of scientist suicides, damn odd hallucinations, all the way to a fantastic virtual reality game that draws intellectuals from around the world before devolving into a suggestive epic space opera featuring some of the most interesting aliens I've read about in a LONG time.<br /><br />The worldbuilding is top-knotch-squared.<br /><br />The clever uses of technology are the true highlights of the novel, and I'm upset. Why? Because the translations and publications for the next two novels are still in the future. Why am I still upset? Because I can hardly find the other works for this great author.<br /><br />A grandmaster of Chinese sci-fi? I can't deny the fact. And just because I can't compare to other science fiction masters of Chinese literature is a null point. I am already a fanboy. I'll be revelling in every work I can get my hands on.<br /><br />This is a fantastic example of how great science fiction can be. Truly inspiring.<br /><br />Update.<br /><br />This novel now a Hugo Nominee for 2015 because of the translation and introduction into the English-speaking market. It is a last minute replacement for Marco Kloos's Lines of Departure that was bravely self-removed due to the Sad Puppy 3 controversy. It wasn't his fault, and he got caught up in some seriously not-cool BS with this year's Hugo. He should be treated like any other Hugo Nominee. With respect and awe for the accomplishment it is, even though he withdrew.<br /><br />On the other hand, after finding out that Three Body Problem took his place, I have to admit that it couldn't have happened to a better novel. I loved this one. It was really fantastic and it had everything I like to see in seriously good fiction.<br /><br />This one might truly be my top pick for the year. It might be the one I cast my ballot on. But first, I need to read a few more Nominees. I take this very seriously. We bring our levels of joy and dedication to the ideas we thrive on. Awards are only as good as we make them. I refuse to let the Hugo become a quagmire.<br /><br />Let the best novel win!<br /><br /><a href="http://danrymes.blogspot.ca/" rel="nofollow noopener">Brad K Horner's Blog</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17946371469530459163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1967162613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1967162613" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1967162613?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781784971571" href="/book/show/26166883-the-three-body-problem">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Hard Sci-Fi fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Petrik by: </span>
      Barack Obama
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1967162613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10332407533191203639"><b>3.5/5 Stars<br/><br/>The Three-Body Problem may be one of the most critically acclaimed Sci-Fi novels of our modern age, and in my opinion, it truly deserved the recognition for all the Sci-Fi ideas and narrative, but not for the characterization.</b><br/><br/>The Three-Body Problem, the first book in <i>Remembrance of Earth’s Past</i> trilogy by Cixin Liu, was actually something I never heard of despite its apparent popularity until I stumbled upon an interview with Barack Obama. He stated that this is one of his favorite no</span>
  <span id="freeText10332407533191203639" style="display:none"><b>3.5/5 Stars<br><br>The Three-Body Problem may be one of the most critically acclaimed Sci-Fi novels of our modern age, and in my opinion, it truly deserved the recognition for all the Sci-Fi ideas and narrative, but not for the characterization.</b><br><br>The Three-Body Problem, the first book in <i>Remembrance of Earth’s Past</i> trilogy by Cixin Liu, was actually something I never heard of despite its apparent popularity until I stumbled upon an interview with Barack Obama. He stated that this is one of his favorite novels, Mark Zuckerberg agreed and said the same thing, and that made me decided to give it a try. Plus, the cover for this trilogy is gorgeous. Most of you probably already know that TTBP won Hugo Award for best novel in the year 2015, it also has been nominated for many other Sci-Fi awards and there’s an upcoming movie in production already.<br><br><b>Pictures: Movie posters of "3Body: Once Upon a Time in Earth". Artworks by Jay Wong</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506263055i/24001654._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506263055i/24001653._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The question is: does it deserved all the praise it received? Mostly yes, especially when it comes to how the book discussed a lot of relatable topics with our current societies; mostly science (d’oh), religions and human nature.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“Even if God were here, it wouldn’t do any good. The entire human race has reached the point where no one is listening to their prayers.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>As far as we know, humans have been reaching for extraterrestrial contact forever now; there’s been plenty of “sightings” or conspiracy theories but none of them are concrete proof. I’m a believer that there are another life forms outside of Earth, and there’s no doubt it will be a groundbreaking discovery when they truly make that first contact with our world. As exciting as that sounds, humans tend to forget that when they do appear, how EXACTLY will humanity react? The Three-Body Problem revolved mostly around this question and the concept of The Three-Body Problem equation—that hasn’t been solved—were used to explore motivation and behavior of humans in the face of the unknown.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“It was impossible to expect a moral awakening from humankind itself, just like it was impossible to expect humans to lift off the earth by pulling up on their own hair. To achieve moral awakening required a force outside the human race.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>The book shone light upon many historical events of China’s Cultural Revolution and several philosophies of the well-known scientists such as Einstein. The entire plot was told in two different timelines, Cultural Revolution and our modern age, both in China; the scope of the story, however, is massive. There’s a lot of limitation to what I can talk about here because the main strength of this book lies within its mystery; telling you more of the plot will definitely change your expectation, in fact, I probably already said more than enough.<br><br>One thing you should definitely know though is that this is a hard Sci-Fi, and I will not claim to understand all the scientific terms in this book. I’m not a genius or science freak here, some scientific terms did go over my head. Don’t get me wrong, I’m quite good at envisioning and understanding Sci-Fi in my head, but only when it comes to something that has to do with pewpewpew (laser gun), ngungggggggg (lightsaber), boom boom wooshhh (space opera) or alternate reality stuff; the terms that go over my head mostly have to do with physics and scientific calculations. For someone who on a good day got 7/100 (don’t you dare laugh at me) on their physics final exam back in high school, stating that I understood all the scientific terms here is equivalent to saying an iguana invented iPhone; impossible. <br><br>Now, enough talking about how dumb and dumber I am with physics, my point is, despite some terms I failed to understand, I was never bored throughout my time reading this book. I don’t think it’s fair for me to judge the prose, especially knowing that this is a translated work but in my opinion, Ken Liu did an excellent job on the translation. Cixin/Ken Liu explained the required terms in understanding the plot as easy as possible, making sure that the readers fully understand the plot at least. The reason I say this book is slightly overrated is not due to the plot or scientific terms; how can I possibly judge something as overrated from something that I don’t fully understand? That’s nonsense. However, it's because of the weak characterizations.<br><br>This book is written in third person limited omniscient narrative and this direction is apt for the story that Cixin Liu tried to tell; with a lot of changes in locations and timelines combined with the withholding of information, they provided a sense of mystery that compelled the reader to continue. However, the downside of this is that the characters felt flat and devoid of feelings because we never truly get inside the person’s head. Characterizations are the most important factors in the books I read, and this book suffers from great characters to love. In fact, I'm fully disconnected from the characters that I can’t even bring myself to care whether they die or not.<br><br><b>Overall, The Three-Body Problem is a great book filled with imaginative ideas and intriguing plot but fell short due to its weak characterizations.</b> If you’re a fan of heavy scientific terms, this is truly a book you must try to read. I can’t wait to see what the sequel has in store because the ending of this book made me realize that this is just a beginning to an epic Sci-Fi tale.<br><br><i>You can find <b><a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/26-pat-reviews/821-thethreebodyproblem" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a></b> and the rest of my Adult Epic/High Fantasy &amp; Sci-Fi reviews at <b><a href="http://booknest.eu/review/patrick" rel="nofollow noopener">BookNest</a></b></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10332407533191203639" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1501022609" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1501022609" />
    <a title="Adina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22189348-adina"><img alt="Adina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531917330p2/22189348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1501022609?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adina" href="/user/show/22189348-adina">Adina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=fantasy-sf">fantasy-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22189348-adina?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1501022609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10617163375448546726">The Three-Body Problem was the best SF novel that I’ve read so far. Admittedly, I did not read a lot of them. However, I can recognize when I encounter a special gem and this one definitely is unique in its world building. Moreover, it is very well written (and translated) which, unfortunately, it is not always the case with SF novels, especially with the classics. <br/><br/>The first chapters take place in the Chinese Cultural revolution and I thought to be a harrowing experience which perfectly introduc</span>
  <span id="freeText10617163375448546726" style="display:none">The Three-Body Problem was the best SF novel that I’ve read so far. Admittedly, I did not read a lot of them. However, I can recognize when I encounter a special gem and this one definitely is unique in its world building. Moreover, it is very well written (and translated) which, unfortunately, it is not always the case with SF novels, especially with the classics. <br /><br />The first chapters take place in the Chinese Cultural revolution and I thought to be a harrowing experience which perfectly introduced the reader in the oppressive atmosphere of the time. I do not want to say too much of the plot because I believe it is better for each of you to explore it. I went in almost blindly and I appreciated the opportunity to discover by myself how the plot develops. What I can tell is that you will read an amazing blend of Chinese history, mythology, hard SCi-Fi and well crayoned characters. If I were to reveal anything I guess this quote from the first part of the novel is pretty suggestive. <br /><br />“It was impossible to expect a moral awakening from humankind itself, just like it was impossible to expect humans to lift off the earth by pulling up on their own hair. To achieve moral awakening required a force outside the human race.”<br /><br />The novel can be hard on science sometimes but the aspect did not lower my enjoyment, although I was overwhelmed by some explanations as my background is mostly economics. Despite some long science passages, the narration flows beautifully and I was not bored for one second. <br /><br />I am looking forward to reading the next volume in the series and I hope it will not suffer from the 2nd books syndrome. <br /><br />**********<br />Excellent! One of the best SF books I've read. Review to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10617163375448546726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2001737886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2001737886" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2001737886?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16355190075980356630">Fascinating piece of scifi by Chinese writer Cixin Liu. A surprising mix of nanoscience, string theory, and astrophysics and religion with the Cultural Revolution as a background, the story takes its protagonist Xiao Wang (the nanoscientist) into an adventure that will impact all of humanity. I liked Ye, the astrophysicist, and found Du Shi, the policeman, funny and well-drawn. As for the action and plot, it is easy to read although I got a little lost in the pure science aspects once or twice (</span>
  <span id="freeText16355190075980356630" style="display:none">Fascinating piece of scifi by Chinese writer Cixin Liu. A surprising mix of nanoscience, string theory, and astrophysics and religion with the Cultural Revolution as a background, the story takes its protagonist Xiao Wang (the nanoscientist) into an adventure that will impact all of humanity. I liked Ye, the astrophysicist, and found Du Shi, the policeman, funny and well-drawn. As for the action and plot, it is easy to read although I got a little lost in the pure science aspects once or twice (despite being an engineer and having dabbled in quantum mechanics years ago). I am excited about reading the next two books (which I suspect will be a little like the Foundation Trilogy by Asimov) and hope you'll also enjoy this one. Note that it won the Hugo award in 2015, kind of a geek's Pulitzer if you will.<br /><br />Having finished the entire series, I have to say that it does actually get better and better as it evolves. The narrative structure of this first book is a quite different than the other two but all are extraordinary.<br />I am reading the Cixin Liu-approved fan extension, The Redemption of Time by Baoshu now, and it is really good but you have to have finished the trilogy to follow it.<br /><br />Fino's Cixin Liu and other Chinese SciFi and Fantasy Reviews<br /><a <span id="freeTextContainer12787860943564309397">A scientist is drawn into a conspiracy involving a computer game and an old research station and extra-terrestrial life.<br/><br/>Translated from the original Chinese. I have to admit I read this book mostly because the way it's being talked about made me really uncomfortable. There's the contingent who want to treat it as some sort of referendum on the Chinese science fiction landscape, or Chinese literature in general, as it was a wildly successful bestseller there.   <span id="freeText12787860943564309397" style="display:none">A scientist is drawn into a conspiracy involving a computer game and an old research station and extra-terrestrial life.<br /><br />Translated from the original Chinese. I have to admit I read this book mostly because the way it's being talked about made me really uncomfortable. There's the contingent who want to treat it as some sort of referendum on the Chinese science fiction landscape, or Chinese literature in general, as it was a wildly successful bestseller there. Yeah, okay, tell you what – go take a look at this week's <i>NY Times</i> bestseller list and pick out the book we should translate into other languages for readers to judge as a referendum on all of American writing of that genre. I'll wait. And then there's the way the translator responded to criticism by making a lot of sweeping statements about Chinese writing that I have very little doubt, even in the absence of any personal expertise, are dubious at best. This book is occupying some weird space in reviewerland, is what I'm saying.<br /><br />So I read it, and. Um. It's not very good. Flat characters, some shall we say eyebrow raising decisions regarding women, a lot of <i>but humans don’t human that way</i>, etc. Which kind of figures, since if notions of <i>best seller</i> can be translated, then this book is Chinese Tom Clancy. So . . . there you go.<br /><br />It did intrigue me on behalf of other Chinese science fiction, though. The cultural context of this story – the asides about how communism impacted intellectual thought, for example – interested me more than anything else.<br /><br />I generally have a pretty good nose for these things, though, and I smell movie deal, for what that's worth.</span>

<div id="review_1084229569" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1084229569" />
    <a title="B Schrodinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger"><img alt="B Schrodinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572325701p2/5448792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1084229569?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B Schrodinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B Schrodinger" href="/user/show/5448792-b-schrodinger">B Schrodinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=physics">physics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=first-contact">first-contact</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5448792-b-schrodinger?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1084229569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2295491228404825225">Originally published in it's native Chinese in 2008, The Three-Body Problem has now been translated for English speakers to read and enjoy. It is the first volume in a hugely successful SF trilogy that has proved to be a popular seller in China.<br/><br/>No matter what our opinions are on the government of China, we all know that they have a history of controlling the media. It was not so long ago that I was reading articles on how even SF stories may not be published if they contain certain themes or SF </span>
  <span id="freeText2295491228404825225" style="display:none">Originally published in it's native Chinese in 2008, The Three-Body Problem has now been translated for English speakers to read and enjoy. It is the first volume in a hugely successful SF trilogy that has proved to be a popular seller in China.<br /><br />No matter what our opinions are on the government of China, we all know that they have a history of controlling the media. It was not so long ago that I was reading articles on how even SF stories may not be published if they contain certain themes or SF tropes that the government does not approve of such as time travel. Yet here we have a novel that Tor are willing to bring to an international English audience. So is it a matter of government restrictions being exaggerated or is it proof that art defies restrictions?<br /><br />While I can think about these questions I do hit a brick wall after a short while. I'm no geography or political buff. I have no ideas on these matters. But I do know quite a bit about SF and I know a little more about science and I feel that these are the most import factors when reading The Three-Body Problem. Sure it would have been great to know what the hell was going on in those early chapters during the 'cultural revolution', but I guess I was lucky to follow the story when it delved a bit into quantum mechanics and orbital mechanics. And while a reader without this knowledge would not have a problem following the story at all and could easily skim those sections, they definitely were rewarding and offered a greater depth to the story. And I'm sure that someone with a knowledge of modern Chinese history would have felt the same.<br /><br />Three-Body is essentially the story of two scientists, Ye Wenjie, an engineer working in a top-secret military base during the 1970's, and Wang Miao, a nanotechnologist in current day China. While events in current day China unfold for Wang, the story of Ye is told in alternate sections. The nature of the top-secret base is uncovered during the intricate story and don't worry, it's not a bad X-Files ripoff at all.<br /><br />But I did find Wang's story much more interesting and frightening. It explores the idea of the failure of science. What happens if over time scientific endeavours consistently defy any conjectures or postulates, refuse to comply with any previously known laws and just keep on giving random and seemingly supernatural outcomes? It may sound a bit trivial here, but the more you think about it, the more frightening it is. And the author explores this and truly did convey the horror to me as the reader. The events of this book had me tense and on-edge at several points.<br /><br />There really are some fascinating ideas pursued in this book and it is a thoroughly entertaining and thought-provoking read in the style of SF greats such as Kim Stanley Robinson and Asomiv. The style of interchanging stories with historical aspects, as well as some of the style did remind me of Murakami, but I have no idea if this is being literature racist as this is the only other Asian book I have read other than those by Murakami. It also had echoes of Neal Stephenson in that it was an intricate and baroque plot full of subterfuges and technical writing. But maybe I'm just projecting two of my favourite authors onto another book that I enjoyed.<br /><br />So here is one reader that is converted to the forthcoming volumes and possibly converted to reading more international SF. Both Stanislaw Lem and the Strugatsky brothers failed to take my interest, but Liu Cixin has managed to produce something that I really did enjoy and also made me think big thoughts.<br /><br /><br /><br />September 2016 Reread:<br /><br />Nothing has changed, but I put my star rating up. It is a special read that I enjoyed just as much the second time around. Yes, there were very little surprises, but I appreciated the pacing and understood more about the characters and the history. <br /><br />I saved my reading of Book 2 and 3 until they were all out. I'm too old and there are too many other books to read to have to reread a series each time a new volume comes out.<br /><br />See you all for for Book 2 review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2295491228404825225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1157053804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1157053804" />
    <a title="Matthew Quann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann"><img alt="Matthew Quann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528393487p2/22699228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1157053804?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Quann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Quann" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann">Matthew Quann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sci-fi fans, Science Nerds, International Reads</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=not-can-us-or-uk">not-can-us-or-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=sff-award-winners">sff-award-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1157053804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2362291507870389372">I just spent a week with this hard science fiction, Hugo-award winning novel from Chinese author Cixin Liu and I have to admit: I'm impressed. <br/><br/><i>The Three-Body Problem</i> had me putting off tasks to pick it up, stuck with me throughout my day, and was always a pleasure to read when I sat down with it. With that said, this isn’t a novel I’d easily recommend to everyone. This isn’t a review that offers a pan-recommendation along with its 5-star rating. Indeed, this review seeks to help an intrigued rea</span>
  <span id="freeText2362291507870389372" style="display:none">I just spent a week with this hard science fiction, Hugo-award winning novel from Chinese author Cixin Liu and I have to admit: I'm impressed. <br /><br /><i>The Three-Body Problem</i> had me putting off tasks to pick it up, stuck with me throughout my day, and was always a pleasure to read when I sat down with it. With that said, this isn’t a novel I’d easily recommend to everyone. This isn’t a review that offers a pan-recommendation along with its 5-star rating. Indeed, this review seeks to help an intrigued reader decide if this book would be a good fit for them and their reading taste. <br /><br /><b>Hard Sci-Fi</b><br /><br /> The premise of <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> is that an alien civilization receives a message from a Chinese scientist in the 1970s and plans to come to Earth, naturally, for a good old-fashioned invasion. <br /><br />I know, I know. You’ve read this story before, right? <br /><br />I assure you that this is a wholly original take wherein the aliens don’t even make a proper appearance for the entire novel. Instead, <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> is more concerned with its titular problem, scientific history, cutting edge scientific theory, and a fair smattering of ludicrous science near the novel’s end. This novel revels in its appreciation of science and a bit of brushing up on introductory physics would not go amiss. <br /><br />However, if you don’t recall the mathematical expression that governs the motion of two celestial bodies in a vacuum, you need not worry. Cixin Liu (and his translator, Ken Liu) does a fantastic job in explaining basic and high-level science concepts in clear language. Although there were times in which I had to set the book down to interpret, these moments were largely towards the end of the book where the science gets really out there. <br /><br />I was also less than impressed with the video game within the book that serves as an introduction to the alien civilization. Roughly, each time the game is booted up the player is greeted by an ever-advancing Earth-based representation of scientific progress. So, at first you meet an ancient Chinese king, but eventually you hang out with Einstein. This grew on me after the first few chapters set in the game. Liu uses these sections to convey the difficulty of the scientific problem at hand, show reverence for science history, and introduce the civilization in an innocuous way. <br /><br /><b>Space &amp; Time</b><br /><br />One of the things that really sets this reading experience apart from traditional science fiction is that it is really, really Chinese. The first 100 pages deal mostly with the Chinese Cultural Revolution. In fact, after 100 pages I realized that I was enjoying that material so much that I wouldn’t have minded if aliens never stopped in. Of course, as the novel goes on it does an excellent job of weaving together the threads from the Cultural Revolution and the impending invasion. <br /><br />This Chinese-based sci-fi is a breath of fresh air, and it’s a shame that China’s most popular sci-fi author has never made the jump to English before. Not only is it set in a different part of the world from most sci-fi you’ll encounter, but it also feels remarkably different in writing style and plot development. Where other novels skim over the nitty-gritty of the science behind spectacle, <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> spends pages making sure the reader knows what to expect. This never feels obnoxious; on the contrary, it is refreshing to see an author convey a concept in such understandable language. <br /><br />Though the novel alternates between the time of discovery during the Chinese Cultural Revolution and the present day story, it never feels random. There are stretches where I spent 50 pages in the present, took a brief 10 page detour into the past, only to return for a lengthy bit set in the present. The story unfolds rather than following a strictly predictable path. Instead of predictability, it seems guided by logic. You don’t know about Y, but once you know X, Y follows much more easily. <br /><br />This all makes for a read that is compelling because it makes the reader feel as if they are hot on the pursuit of the central mystery. <br /><br /><b>The Three Dimensional Character Problem</b><br /><br />You’ve probably noticed that I haven’t mentioned the cast of characters, and there’s good reason. I’m not the first reader to note this, but the lead characters in <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> are pretty flat. Indeed, Wang Miao may be a brilliant nanotechnology researcher, but I’d be hard pressed to tell you much about his personality. Instead of driving the plot, Wang reacts to it. I never felt that the decisions he makes in the novel were guided by his belief system. He’s kind of like the cart on an on-rail amusement park ride. The ride sure is thrilling, but you’re unlikely to remember much about the cart.<br /><br />Now, normally I’d be roasting this book alive for having such weak characterization. I mean, why read a book if you don’t care about the characters? <i>The Three-Body Problem</i> genuinely makes the case for having a fairly empty lead. I kept thinking during my read that I could imagine being in Wang’s shoes, pulling back the curtain on yet another mystery. It is genuinely impressive that Liu is able to pull off, at least for me, the sensation of feeling like you’re in on the mystery that would be lost with a stronger character. <br /><br /><b>Conclusion?</b><br /><br />This novel doesn’t end with resolution, though you could conceivably just read this novel and come away with a complete story. Of course, there are two more novels in the series that will delve further into the impressive, exciting, and pessimistic world that Liu has created. I’m hoping for some better-developed characters, but will happily continue on if the subsequent books are as mentally stimulating as this.<br /><br /><b>I’d suggest tackling this one if you are interested in a headier science-fiction story that eschews typical western plot, makes your brain twist and turn into weird shapes, and makes the case for more translated SFF.</b><br /><br />**The second book is better than the first! You can find my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1805158377?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>The Dark Forest</i> here!</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2362291507870389372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1062421201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062421201" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1143969-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409947508p2/1143969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062421201?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/1143969-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18245960-the-three-body-problem">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=asia-or-aapi">asia-or-aapi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=non-white-mc">non-white-mc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=non-western-setting">non-western-setting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=not-my-cup-of-tea">not-my-cup-of-tea</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=hugo-awards-2015">hugo-awards-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=virtual-reality">virtual-reality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1062421201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11165221104199569258">I'm really waffling between whether to rate "did not like it" vs. "it was okay" -- I very, very rarely give out one stars, and it feels uncharitable because it was a book I <i>wanted</i> to like more than I did, and I want more diverse SF, but... no. I've consciously created a "not my cup of tea" shelf for this very book, however, because a lot of people seem to have liked it. Is this what hard SF is like? In which case, it reminds me of similar "I am completely unable to get interested in this" proble</span>
  <span id="freeText11165221104199569258" style="display:none">I'm really waffling between whether to rate "did not like it" vs. "it was okay" -- I very, very rarely give out one stars, and it feels uncharitable because it was a book I <i>wanted</i> to like more than I did, and I want more diverse SF, but... no. I've consciously created a "not my cup of tea" shelf for this very book, however, because a lot of people seem to have liked it. Is this what hard SF is like? In which case, it reminds me of similar "I am completely unable to get interested in this" problems I had with Kim Stanley Robinson last year.<br /><br />I actually started this book months ago, but wasn't feeling it after the first chapter and stopped. I picked it up again now, stubbornly ploughing through because of the Hugos, and I kept waiting for it to suddenly turn around and wow me, but... it never did. At the 80% mark, I was still waiting.<br /><br />Learning more about the Chinese Cultural Revolution was fascinating, and I liked seeing its colossal effects on Ye, plus the feeling of 'science will save us' that permeates the society. Liu Cixin's imagination of an alien society was really good and unique (dehydrate! dehydrate!).<br /><br />Da Shi is, hands-down, the best character of this entire book. I much rather wanted to read his tales of fighting crime, with his seedy, no-bullshit, 'I'm not a <i>good</i> cop, but I'm a great cop' approach. He livened up every scene he was in! Instead, this was so much like reading a physics textbook.<br /><br />That's about where my praise ends, because I prefer emotional character-driven plots with some action, whereas this is a science-driven impersonal plod. Who the hell is Wang, our protagonist? After one single scene with his wife and son (!), they literally disappear for the rest of the book, and I couldn't tell you what his personality is like. He's just the viewfinder through which we see information unfold -- and unfold it does, with just reams and reams of exposition and info-dumps.<br /><br />The prose is dull. I didn't so much mind it being stilted, and the dialogue carrying the remnants of its original language (a conscious effort on the translator Ken Liu's part), but it's just <i>such</i> a trudging plod. I highlighted a few more poetic passages that I really liked, but for the most part it leans more to clinical and dry.<br /><br />I <i>really</i> liked the virtual reality chapters, but after all that buildup, I feel like it just fizzles out and absolutely, literally, nothing has been accomplished by the end of the book. With where the plot goes, the entire book honestly just feels like a prologue for the sequel.<br /><br />I feel like the Goodreads blurb was pretty awfully off-base, touting that it has "the scope of <i>Dune</i> and the commercial action of <i>Independence Day</i>". I... what??? There is literally ONE action scene, it occurs about 90% through the book, and the characters aren't actively involved, just watching on from afar. And while the alien world/society is interestingly-written, it is nowhere near the scope of Dune.<br /><br />Without characters with real depth to get attached to, I just never got hooked into this book. Ye has so much potential, but I feel like she wasn't fleshed out enough either -- people's more interesting psychological choices are left unplumbed/unexplored, meaning that I'm left with behaviour that I don't really buy, that doesn't seem natural for a human to do. Specifically: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I'm talking about the rebels turning against all of humanity, to the extent of campaigning for the deaths of <i>themselves</i> and <i>their own children</i>. And this is a widespread movement?? We're such a selfish, self-centered, survival-oriented species that I just don't believe it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The chronology and pacing is all over the place, too, hopping back and forth in time and as characters tell each other rambling stories. It's slow and sedate and takes forever to get anywhere -- the blurb trumps up <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the alien invasion <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but even that doesn't happen in the book at all, and indeed, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[won't actually happen for another 400+ years! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, so what in the world is the point of this story?<br /><br />I mean, I get the point. I get that it's about humans pitted against humans, and the divisive cracks that can tear us apart even without the physical presence of an Other.<br /><br />But man, I just couldn't bring myself to care. I'm so sorry, Cixin. I wanted to love it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11165221104199569258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1108936911" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1108936911" />
    <a title="David Brin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3018638-david-brin"><img alt="David Brin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352955793p2/3018638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1108936911?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Brin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Brin" href="/user/show/3018638-david-brin">David Brin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1108936911" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3736301921963677492">The Three-Body Problem is part one of an award-winning trilogy by Liu Cixin — and is arguably the best Chinese science fiction novel ever translated into English. Liu uses the “three-body problem” of classical mechanics to ask some terrifying questions about human nature and what lies at the core of civilization.<br/><br/>The series explores the world of the Trisolarans, a race that is forced to adapt to life in a triple star system, on a planet whose gravity, heat, and orbit are in constant flux. Facing </span>
  <span id="freeText3736301921963677492" style="display:none">The Three-Body Problem is part one of an award-winning trilogy by Liu Cixin — and is arguably the best Chinese science fiction novel ever translated into English. Liu uses the “three-body problem” of classical mechanics to ask some terrifying questions about human nature and what lies at the core of civilization.<br /><br />The series explores the world of the Trisolarans, a race that is forced to adapt to life in a triple star system, on a planet whose gravity, heat, and orbit are in constant flux. Facing extinction, the Trisolarans plan to evacuate and conquer the nearest habitable planet, and finally chooses a candidate/victim when it intercepts a message—from Earth. The Three-Body Problem has been translated into English by award-winning writer, Ken Liu (author of books such as The Grace of Kings). Take a look at Stephan Martiniere's way-cool cover for the coming Tor Books edition!)<br /><br />Special note… The Three Body Problem deals very closely with the issue of SETI and the Fermi Paradox and whether we should shout "yoo-hoo!" into the cosmos -- a quandary about which I've also written, from time to time. <br />￼<br /><br />But the biggest news is this proof of the maturation of Chinese science fiction into the top ranks of thorough and fascinating thought experimentation. I’ve long maintained that the health of an enlightened and progressive society is measured by how vibrant is its science fiction, since that is where true self-critique and appraisal and hope lie. If so, the good news stretches beyond China!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3736301921963677492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1367790472" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1367790472" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1367790472?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1367790472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7527351776610938326">I liked this and there is no doubt that this is a science heavy, brilliantly produced and contemplated, highly original SF novel from a physics understanding Chinese author that was good enough to win a slew of awards including the Hugo.<br/><br/>But I like to watch Ridiculousness. I like Travis McGee. I like Mickey F****** Spillane. Beer and pizza and a bug zapper is quality entertainment.<br/><br/>“Conan – what is best in life?”<br/><br/>Three Body Problem did not have near enough axe welding barbarians or laser beams for </span>
  <span id="freeText7527351776610938326" style="display:none">I liked this and there is no doubt that this is a science heavy, brilliantly produced and contemplated, highly original SF novel from a physics understanding Chinese author that was good enough to win a slew of awards including the Hugo.<br><br>But I like to watch Ridiculousness. I like Travis McGee. I like Mickey F****** Spillane. Beer and pizza and a bug zapper is quality entertainment.<br><br>“Conan – what is best in life?”<br><br>Three Body Problem did not have near enough axe welding barbarians or laser beams for my taste.<br><br>Liu Cixin’s wildly popular, speculative fiction gem was first published in China in 2008 as 三体 (I guess – that’s what it says on Goodreads) and I enjoyed the English translation written by Ken Liu (himself a very talented writer) published in 2014.<br><br>Liu begins in the late 60s during the Chinese Cultural Revolution and then moves forward in time to a near future where a strange virtual reality game replicates an even stranger reality.<br><br>And the aliens.<br><br>Maybe I would have liked this more if I were a physicist. A very smart commentator opined that perhaps Westerners don’t get the subtle nuances of a writer whose cultural background is not based upon the individual but rather is more attuned to a gestalt perspective.<br><br>“Brilliant and entertaining “ <br>– Dr. Sheldon Cooper.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464748078ra/19271152.gif" width="400" height="216" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="7527351776610938326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1612803050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1612803050" />
    <a title="Manuel Antão" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28709846-manuel-ant-o"><img alt="Manuel Antão" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1504271107p2/28709846.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1612803050?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manuel Antão" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manuel Antão" href="/user/show/28709846-manuel-ant-o">Manuel Antão</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18245960-the-three-body-problem">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28709846-manuel-ant-o?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1612803050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6443807455226908937">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2016/04/simple-minded-sf-three-body-problem-by.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/>Simple-Minded SF: "The Three-Body Problem" by Liu Cixin, Ken Liu (translator)<br/><br/><br/>"The thin curve [when Ye was watching a waveform on a screen supposedly from an alien civilization], rising and falling, seemed to possess a soul."<br/><br/>In "The Three-Body Problem" by Liu Cixin, Ken Liu (translator)<br/><br/> <br/>Metaphor only takes me so far...When I’m reading a supposedly hard SF book I must put into action my non-suspension-of-disbelief-hat. That’s the only wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6443807455226908937" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="http://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2016/04/simple-minded-sf-three-body-problem-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br />Simple-Minded SF: "The Three-Body Problem" by Liu Cixin, Ken Liu (translator)<br /><br /><br />"The thin curve [when Ye was watching a waveform on a screen supposedly from an alien civilization], rising and falling, seemed to possess a soul."<br /><br />In "The Three-Body Problem" by Liu Cixin, Ken Liu (translator)<br /><br /> <br />Metaphor only takes me so far...When I’m reading a supposedly hard SF book I must put into action my non-suspension-of-disbelief-hat. That’s the only way I can read this kind of SF. I’ve heard from some friends of mine, that some books are all metaphor when the physics part of them are utter crap…. Excuse me? It's like saying, "look here, this is my universe, but try not to concentrate too much on it, look at all the beautiful metaphors I wrote instead." Don’t tell me this is me being pedantic. One thing is getting the physics right from scratch, the other thing is to do the extrapolation stuff the “right” way. In this case, base physics is quite off base, i.e., dead wrong in several key areas of the book. They’re so wrong that I only finished it because I wanted to pin-point the rest of the so-called errors. I know, I’m mean…The above-mentioned example is one of the most glaring examples. A wave form where one’s able to see something behind it just by looking at it! Even with poetical license in play, this is quite a bit of a stretch. I could mention another examples, but this one is one of the most obvious examples in showing that Cixin’s storytelling leaves a lot to be desired.<br /> <br />If you're into crappy SF, read the rest elsewhere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6443807455226908937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
